    <div class="span6" id="primary" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Article">
     <div id="content" role="main">
      <article class="post-8437 post type-post status-publish format-standard has-post-thumbnail hentry category-bytes tag-classic-rock tag-eric-clapton tag-layla entry-page" id="post-8437">
       <div class="single-post ">
        <div class="media-thumb img-format">
         <img alt="The Unusual History of Layla  The Single" src="http://www.udiscovermusic.com/wp-content/uploads/2014/08/Layla1.jpg"/>
         <meta content="http://www.udiscovermusic.com/wp-content/uploads/2014/08/Layla1.jpg" itemprop="image"/>
        </div>
        <!-- /media-thumb -->
        <h1 class="entry-title" itemprop="name" style="display:none;">
         The Unusual History of Layla  The Single
        </h1>
        <div class="entry-content" itemprop="articleBody">
         <p>
          The story of
          <strong>
           <a href="http://www.udiscovermusic.com/artists/eric-clapton" target="_blank">
            Derek and the Dominos
           </a>
          </strong>
          , Layla and Other Assorted Love Songs is not all it seems. Neither the album, whose title is taken from the track Layla, nor the outstanding single, that is now considered one of rocks greatest love songs, do nearly as well when they were originally released as many of us imagine.
         </p>
         <p>
          The album came out in November 1970, and in America made the charts later that month entering at 195, going on to peak at No.16, probably not as high a chart placing as many would guess, if asked. In the UK Layla and Other Assorted Love Songs failed to chart at all, no thats not quite true. It finally made No.68 on the album charts in 2011 when it was reissued.
         </p>
         <p>
          <img alt="Original Layla Ad_edited-1" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-8439" height="724" src="http://www.udiscovermusic.com/wp-content/uploads/2014/08/Original-Layla-Ad_edited-1.jpg" width="530"/>
          <br/>
          The review in Britains Melody Maker was somewhat reserved in its praise for the album, stating that If you do judge Derek and the Dominos by
          <strong>
           <a href="http://www.udiscovermusic.com/artists/cream" target="_blank">
            Cream
           </a>
          </strong>
          standards youll be disappointed. It has as the years have rolled by become increasingly popular and many now regard this as Eric Claptons masterpiece. Layla and Other Assorted Love Songs has been inducted into the Grammy Hall of Fame, and Rolling Stone ranked it number 117 on its list of The 500 Greatest Albums of All Time.
         </p>
         <p>
          But what about Layla as a single? In the US it was released as an edited 45rpm in March 1971 and made No.51 in the charts: Bell Bottom Blues was the lead single from the album. A year later a longer version was issued in the US and it fared a lot better, entering the Hot 100 at No.92 on 13 May 1972 it reached No.10 on the Billboard charts in early August. In the UK it was not released at all until 1 August 1972, and only then in the shortened version (barely 2 and three quarter minutes long). It made No.7 on the charts in 1972 and a decade later it charted again, making No.4 in 1982.
         </p>
         <p>
          The Melody Makers July 1972 review of Layla
         </p>
         <p>
          <img alt="720729 Layla review_edited-1" class="aligncenter size-full wp-image-8440" height="582" src="http://www.udiscovermusic.com/wp-content/uploads/2014/08/720729-Layla-review_edited-1.jpg" width="530"/>
         </p>
         <p>
          <iframe frameborder="0" height="380" src="https://embed.spotify.com/?uri=spotify:album:79u2ZsF3EcnpDO29gBfIQu" width="300">
          </iframe>
         </p>
        </div>
        <!-- .entry-content -->
       </div>

           <article class="comment clearfix" id="comment-1611">
            <header class="comment-meta comment-author vcard">
             <div class="comment-avatar">
              <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-75 photo" height="75" src="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/12970aa7251c141625bdd34207545ee0?s=75&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F1.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D75&amp;r=G" width="75"/>
             </div>
             <strong>
              <a class="url" href="http://wordpresswiz.net" rel="external nofollow">
               Michael Gorman
              </a>
             </strong>
             <br/>
             <a class="comment-meta-time muted-small" href="http://www.udiscovermusic.com/real-history-layla#comment-1611">
              <time datetime="2014-08-01T16:40:37+00:00">
               August 1, 2014 at 4:40 pm
              </time>
             </a>
             <a class="comment-reply-link" href="/real-history-layla?replytocom=1611#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm("comment-1611", "1611", "respond", "8437")'>
              Reply
             </a>
             
            </header>
            <!-- .comment-meta -->
            <section class="comment-content comment">
             <p>
              Yes, I recall in the later 70s reading that Eric Clapton was very depressed at the first reception of the album, which after all was quite an innovative and unusual format for the times  you could say ahead of its time as a conceptual album of bluesy love ballads and melodic guitar playing  it was just not really appreciated until it had some time and skin in the game . This is so often the case with rich offerings from artists, they are just not appreciated for a while until folks live with it and grow to love it. I recall not liking Dark side of The Moon when i first heard it, later of course the penny dropped and it became a classic.
             </p>
            </section>

            <!-- .comment-meta -->
            <section class="comment-content comment">
             <p>
              From first listen as a young teen I was captivated by the haunting vocals, the cool riff, the slide playing and the 180 degree turn to the beautifully executed part II of the title song. The pangs of love and loss that eminated from those songs struck a chord with this boy and still does over 40 years later!
             </p>
            </section>

             </a>
             
            </header>
            <!-- .comment-meta -->
            <section class="comment-content comment">
             <p>
              Was only 11 when the album was released, but bought the single in 72 and remembering hearing Layla played on the radio non-stop. Ive had the CD for at least 20 years and listen to it a lot. If I recall, the combination of lack of commercial success of the album and Claptons unrequited love, led to Claptons heroin addiction and self-imposed exile until the Rainbow Concert. 2 years today is nothing, but back then it was a lifetime.
             </p>
            </section>

              <section class="comment-content comment">
               <p>
                Yawn.Jim Gordon dated Rita Coolidge as she dated many rockers in her day.   Supposedly, the piano piece was taken from a ditty that the members of Delaney &amp; Bonnie used to play after gigs.   Jim recorded the piece one night and Clapton liked it.   Priscilla Coolidge was married to Booker T. Jones and recorded it as Time after Layla.   End of Story.
               </p>

               <p>
                Couldnt agree less. I thought it was a huge letdown after Layla. Duane was gone and Clapton was heavy into smack at that point. The performances were largely boring and none of them measured up to the studio cuts. That album was the beginning of Claptons long slide from brilliance to mediocrity that lasted throughout the 70s. The fiery Clapton of Mayall, Cream, Blind Faith, and Layla was gone forever.
               </p>
               <p>
                I hate that album.
               </p>

                 <div class="comment-avatar">
                  <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-75 photo" height="75" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/899f5a8ced16dcf89fe9f2a553347c5d?s=75&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F0.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D75&amp;r=G" width="75"/>

                   Todd Wolfe

                 <a class="comment-meta-time muted-small" href="http://www.udiscovermusic.com/real-history-layla#comment-1754">
                  <time datetime="2014-08-04T04:04:58+00:00">
                   August 4, 2014 at 4:04 am
                  </time>
                 </a>
                 <a class="comment-reply-link" href="/real-history-layla?replytocom=1754#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm("comment-1754", "1754", "respond", "8437")'>
                  Reply
                 </a>
                 
                </header>
                <!-- .comment-meta -->
                <section class="comment-content comment">
                 <p>
                  Actually Just Bill, hard to compare as Layla was really a masterpiece and a studio album and Live was just one of the stops along the road for The Dominos. I loved it when it first came out and still do. Unfortunately when they re-packaged the release the replaced some of the better versions of songs for example why does love have to be so sad with another version that was not as good and they included extra tracks that were not on the album when first released that should not have been released. The Dominos shows varied and some were much heavier that the Fillmore nights such as The Electric Factory in Philadelphia. They actually toured Europe before coming to the USA to record the album. Many of the shows are available as bootlegs but unfortunately the quality of the recordings are poor but some of the playing and performances are riveting and Clapton displays plenty of fire. I reckon you havent seen Clapton live through the years because of seen plenty of shows where he reaches deep and theres plenty of fire in his playing. Check out the bootleg from Irving Plaza in NYC during his blues tour in support of the release from the cradle after his acoustic portion for the first third of the show he then went electric and then lit it up. Yes there have been tours where he has laid back a bit but plenty through the late eighties through the nineties where Eric displayed plenty of fire.
                 </p>
                </section>

             </a>

            <!-- .comment-meta -->
            <section class="comment-content comment">
             <p>
              In the early 70s I was living on my working Narrow Boats &amp; got out of touch with music trends &amp; relied on juke boxes in pubs . I walked up to Daventry from the canal to meet a chick &amp; while waiting for her in pub I fed juke box repetitively playing  American Pie  &amp;  Layla  ~ When chick turned up I said  who the  f- Deric &amp; the Dominoes ?  ~  How come you dont know ?  she  said ~  no wonder its great  I said when she explained . Both these records remain my favourites &amp; when I play them 40 + years on they make me smile &amp; great memories of those care free days.
             </p>
            </section>

          <li class="comment odd alt thread-even depth-1" id="li-comment-1640">
           <article class="comment clearfix" id="comment-1640">
            <header class="comment-meta comment-author vcard">
             <div class="comment-avatar">
              <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-75 photo" height="75" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/c65d366561570ad137ecd687c59c003c?s=75&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F0.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D75&amp;r=G" width="75"/>
             </div>
             <strong>
              Mike
             </strong>
             <br/>
             <a class="comment-meta-time muted-small" href="http://www.udiscovermusic.com/real-history-layla#comment-1640">
              <time datetime="2014-08-01T21:18:37+00:00">
               August 1, 2014 at 9:18 pm
              </time>
             </a>
             <a class="comment-reply-link" href="/real-history-layla?replytocom=1640#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm("comment-1640", "1640", "respond", "8437")'>
              Reply
             </a>
             
            </header>
            <!-- .comment-meta -->
            <section class="comment-content comment">
             <p>
              I saw Duane play at the Atlanta Pop Festival in early July 1970. He was at the time going back and forth between Macon and Miami for recording sessions with Derek and the Dominoes. Though I didnt know it until years later when the Allman Brothers released their Atlanta Pop Festival gigs on CD, Duane threw in the signature Layla riff in his Whipping Post solo. Of course, not a single soul recognized it at the time. What a moment captured forever!
             </p>
            </section>
            <!-- .comment-content -->
           </article>

           <article class="comment clearfix" id="comment-1648">
            <header class="comment-meta comment-author vcard">
             <div class="comment-avatar">
              <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-75 photo" height="75" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/eb910ab7b611b4dbd3e4e41c7a333a37?s=75&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F0.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D75&amp;r=G" width="75"/>
             </div>
             <strong>
              LT Jaeger
             </strong>
             <br/>
             <a class="comment-meta-time muted-small" href="http://www.udiscovermusic.com/real-history-layla#comment-1648">
              <time datetime="2014-08-01T22:21:01+00:00">
               August 1, 2014 at 10:21 pm
              </time>
             </a>
             <a class="comment-reply-link" href="/real-history-layla?replytocom=1648#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm("comment-1648", "1648", "respond", "8437")'>
              Reply
             </a>
             
            </header>
            <!-- .comment-meta -->
            <section class="comment-content comment">
             <p>
              The raw emotion coming not only from Erics voice, but, especially, from the literally screaming guitars of him and Duane Allman just cant be topped.  Besides the title track, the riffs on Have You Ever Loved a Woman, Why Does Love Got To Be So Sad, and many other tracks come for the heart and soul of the artists.
             </p>
             <p>
              Someday someone might match it, but itll never be topped for pure heart-wrenching emotive playing.
             </p>
            </section>

             <a class="comment-reply-link" href="/real-history-layla?replytocom=1655#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm("comment-1655", "1655", "respond", "8437")'>
              Reply
             </a>
             
            </header>
            <!-- .comment-meta -->
            <section class="comment-content comment">
             <p>
              We all know the back story of the title song. He loved the wife of his best friend, George Harrison, but thought it could never be. He read the Persian (Iranian) love poem (its almost a hundred pages long in the form i read) of unrequited love and wrote of his love for Patti. I totally understand the suffering he would have been going through but here just wanted to say that as good as Layla the albums are, dont you think his playing on the live Derek &amp; set is about the most passionate he ever released for us to hear? His heartache oozes out of every riff. I feel guilty that i like those recordings so much when he was suffering so much but i vote that as his best playing to recording ever.
             </p>
            </section>
            <!-- .comment-content -->
           </article>
           <!-- #comment-## -->
          </li>
          <!-- #comment-## -->
          <li class="comment even thread-even depth-1" id="li-comment-1657">
           <article class="comment clearfix" id="comment-1657">
            <header class="comment-meta comment-author vcard">
             <div class="comment-avatar">
              <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-75 photo" height="75" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/c1854130262bcbecc0c2be3170414988?s=75&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F0.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D75&amp;r=G" width="75"/>
             </div>
             <strong>
              Mike Eagen
             </strong>
             <br/>
             <a class="comment-meta-time muted-small" href="http://www.udiscovermusic.com/real-history-layla#comment-1657">
              <time datetime="2014-08-01T23:38:10+00:00">
               August 1, 2014 at 11:38 pm
              </time>
             </a>
             <a class="comment-reply-link" href="/real-history-layla?replytocom=1657#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm("comment-1657", "1657", "respond", "8437")'>
              Reply
             </a>
             
            </header>
            <!-- .comment-meta -->
            <section class="comment-content comment">
             <p>
              As someone pointed out, Jim Gordons beautiful piano coda was added, according to producer Tom Dodd in an interview I saw on A&amp;E or one of those cable stations a few years back, nearly two weeks after the initial recording of the main piece. One of the great session drummers of all time, he was clearly a fine multi-instrumentalist and composer.
             </p>
             <p>
              Sadly, in addition to Duane Allmans death not that long after the recording of Layla, a little over ten years later, Gordon, who was an un-diagnosed paranoid schizophrenic, murdered his mother with a claw hammer after the voices told him to. Even though both defense and prosecution stipulated to the fact that he was clearly very ill, he was convicted of second degree murder in 1984 and sentenced to 16 years to life. What most people dont realize is that when there is this medically recognized psychiatric aspect to the crime, sentences like the one Jim received are essentially open ended. Had he been just the average Joe Sixpack who snapped one night and killed Mom, hed have been out by now. With his condition, even though medicated, he will most likely remain a guest of the State of California to his dying day. A real tragedy for all concerned, including his poor mother obviously.
             </p>
            </section>

At the time of the records release, I favored Erics musical ideas, but these days I think Duanes were stronger.

          <li class="comment odd alt thread-even depth-1" id="li-comment-1661">
           <article class="comment clearfix" id="comment-1661">
            <header class="comment-meta comment-author vcard">
             <div class="comment-avatar">
              <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-75 photo" height="75" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/058499e5f2ba40ac520caafe4073e1ef?s=75&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F0.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D75&amp;r=G" width="75"/>
             </div>
             <strong>
              Blues Lover
             </strong>
             <br/>
             <a class="comment-meta-time muted-small" href="http://www.udiscovermusic.com/real-history-layla#comment-1661">
              <time datetime="2014-08-02T00:17:36+00:00">
               August 2, 2014 at 12:17 am
              </time>
             </a>
             <a class="comment-reply-link" href="/real-history-layla?replytocom=1661#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm("comment-1661", "1661", "respond", "8437")'>
              Reply
             </a>
             
            </header>
            <!-- .comment-meta -->
            <section class="comment-content comment">
             <p>
              Missed some of the good stuff.  Tom Dowd was shocked when Eric showed up with a strat and small fender amp, last time he recorded with him he played a gibson and had multiple marshall stacks.  Things were not going well in the beginning.  The ABB were in town playing an outdoor concert so Eric went to see them.  He went under the stage and popped up right in front.  According to Betts, when he saw him he turned his back to the audience so Eric could not see him play.  After the concert he invited them all to Criteria Studios, which is how Duane ended up playing on the record, he was very excited about playing with Eric. The intro to Key to the Highway is a fade in.  In the studio next to Erics was Sam the Sham and the Pharaohs who were playing the song, Eric and Duane heard it and joined in, it was so good, Dowd started recording it, hence the fade in. Clapton wrote later in his autobiography that he and Allman were inseparable during the sessions in Florida; he talked about Allman as the musical brother Id never had but wished I did.
             </p>
            </section>
            <!-- .comment-content -->
           </article>

              Anyday is my all time favorite song by far. Tp me it is a musical masterpiece with all of the guitar and drum licks throughout the entire song. There is so much going on within this song at all times instrument wise.

              Clapton and Allman will remain in my world as the greatest complimenting guitar duo that ever walked the planet.             <section class="comment-content comment">
             <p>

            <!-- .comment-meta -->
            <section class="comment-content comment">
             <p>
              I remember when the Layla album came out.  I was in the Army at Ft. Carson, CO.  The album was huge with everyone I knew on the post  including even the black G.I.s. who normally stuck to their soul music.   Everyone loved it.  Especially Layla.  It was always playing in the barracks and on the local radio stations.  I prefer his bluesy music more than the big pop hits like After Midnight and Wonderful Tonight. Me and Mr. Johnson is one of my faves.  His jam with Steve Winwood on Dear Mr. Fantasy (on youtube) is exquisite.  It can bring tears to my eyes.  Eric is not God.  He actually plays guitar better than God.
             </p>
            </section>

          <li class="comment even thread-odd thread-alt depth-1" id="li-comment-1694">
           <article class="comment clearfix" id="comment-1694">
            <header class="comment-meta comment-author vcard">
             <div class="comment-avatar">
              <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-75 photo" height="75" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/42cd47675a6a255f1a82b248abe0783c?s=75&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F0.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D75&amp;r=G" width="75"/>
             </div>
             <strong>
              Mick Lazer
             </strong>
             <br/>
             <a class="comment-meta-time muted-small" href="http://www.udiscovermusic.com/real-history-layla#comment-1694">
              <time datetime="2014-08-02T06:38:16+00:00">
               August 2, 2014 at 6:38 am
              </time>
             </a>
             <a class="comment-reply-link" href="/real-history-layla?replytocom=1694#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm("comment-1694", "1694", "respond", "8437")'>
              Reply
             </a>
             
            </header>
            <!-- .comment-meta -->
            <section class="comment-content comment">
             <p>
              Although the song Layla was extremely well done, it appears to be, for the most part, a cover of a song recorded by Bill Grahams Fillmore Records band Sawbuck released in  the summer of 1970 and written by William Mojo Collins. The song I am talking about, is called Promised Land. In the summer of 1970, Clapton was playing Winterland in San Francisco and was subsequently invited to the record release party. He left with a copy under his arm. Mojo has stated that he did a great job with it, but I think that the record should be set straight about who actually wrote those riffs.  Although Eric Clapton is one of the greatest guitarists ever, and did many unsurpassed arrangements of other peoples songs, he was never a prolific writer of songs. I think that borrowing peoples songs is okay, if that writer gets the credit they deserve! Sadly, this is one case where it did not happen.  Mick Lazer
             </p>
            </section>
            <!-- .comment-content -->
           </article>

                <!-- .comment-meta -->
                <section class="comment-content comment">
                 <p>
                  Sawbucks album was recorded and mixed in July of 1971.  The album was released in 1972over a year after Layla was released.  I think it is safe to say that Ronnie Montrose (who was a huge fan of Claptons at the time) was under the spell of Layla when he recorded Promised Land.  As for the lick in question, its an Albert King lick from As the Years Go Passing By that Duane Allman sped up.
                 </p>
                </section>

             <a class="comment-reply-link" href="/real-history-layla?replytocom=1719#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm("comment-1719", "1719", "respond", "8437")'>
              Reply
             </a>
             
            </header>
            <!-- .comment-meta -->
            <section class="comment-content comment">
             <p>
              I lived and breathed this album for years. It defined my life about the time it was released. I have it on CD now but the sound is not the same as the original vinyl. It lacks the original omph of the vinyl sound which is true, I suppose, about most music that was originally released on vinyl. Nevertheless, it is record that grows on you. I especially loved what Eric &amp; Greg did to Jimi Hendrixs Little Wing. I enjoy both versions equally which is something I cant say about too many remakes of original material.
             </p>
            </section>

            <!-- .comment-meta -->
            <section class="comment-content comment">
             <p>
              Found the Layla single in 1972 for 50 pence English. Sold for the price of the album. Blown away by the music. It got me to buy Live at Fillmore East by the Allman Brothers Band. After 40 years I am still buying Clapton and going to his concerts. Have studio, archive and instant live recordings of the Allmans and have just purchased the 6 disc Fillmore recordings. It shows how one track has influence my life time of listing to music. My favourite album ever .
             </p>
            </section>

            <!-- .comment-meta -->
            <section class="comment-content comment">
             <p>
              From the first time I heard Layla I said repeatedly that it was one song that could never be covered, even by Clapton because there was no longer a Duane Allman to add the necessary soul that was in the original. I do have to admit that I was wrong. Clapton did a masterful job of completely re-inventing the song with the re-release and while it was totally different it was still a very good version and I am glad he did it.
             </p>
            </section>

             </p>

              What Led Zepellin, Guns n Roses, Kiss, and the like produce is bad noise which can be called music but its actually just ugly.  There are folks out there that do something similar but actually sound good:  AC/DC, ZZ Top, Def Leppard, etc.
             </p>

           <article class="comment clearfix" id="comment-1732">
            <header class="comment-meta comment-author vcard">
             <div class="comment-avatar">
              <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-75 photo" height="75" src="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/d8b37adde4335cf444e411e69b5b5718?s=75&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F1.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D75&amp;r=G" width="75"/>
             </div>
             <strong>
              Jim
             </strong>
             <br/>
             <a class="comment-meta-time muted-small" href="http://www.udiscovermusic.com/real-history-layla#comment-1732">
              <time datetime="2014-08-03T05:31:46+00:00">
               August 3, 2014 at 5:31 am
              </time>
             </a>
             <a class="comment-reply-link" href="/real-history-layla?replytocom=1732#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm("comment-1732", "1732", "respond", "8437")'>
              Reply
             </a>
             
            </header>
            <!-- .comment-meta -->
            <section class="comment-content comment">
             <p>
              I saw Derek and the Dominoes at Suffolk County Community College on Long Island N.Y. in the fall of 1970. Ive been a big fan of Clapton since his Yardbird days. Clapton had just left Cream and Blind Faith and when Derek and the Dominoes was formed Clapton wanted to originally be anonymous within the band. He was tired of being the center of attention. Thats why his name isnt prominent in the bands name or on the album. I believe thats why the sales of the album were a bit off early on. Unless you followed Claptons career closely at the time many people didnt realize he was part of Derek and the Dominoes or that Duane Allman was such an important part of the recording. Some 40+ yrs later its hard to find a rock and roll fan thats not familiar with Layla.
             </p>
            </section>

           <article class="comment clearfix" id="comment-8994">
            <header class="comment-meta comment-author vcard">
             <div class="comment-avatar">
              <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-75 photo" height="75" src="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/76f8dfdef8c8e74246ac7850d9a123b7?s=75&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F1.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D75&amp;r=G" width="75"/>
             </div>
             <strong>
              <a class="url" href="http://kdauphinee.com" rel="external nofollow">
               Kevin
              </a>
             </strong>
             <br/>
             <a class="comment-meta-time muted-small" href="http://www.udiscovermusic.com/real-history-layla#comment-8994">
              <time datetime="2015-03-03T20:27:22+00:00">
               March 3, 2015 at 8:27 pm
              </time>
             </a>
             <a class="comment-reply-link" href="/real-history-layla?replytocom=8994#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm("comment-8994", "8994", "respond", "8437")'>
              Reply
             </a>
             
            </header>
            <!-- .comment-meta -->
            <section class="comment-content comment">
             <p>
              While stationed in Pearl Harbor in 1970, we learned from KPOI-FM that LAOALS was going to be released, featuring EC. Picked up the double LP the day it was released &amp; took it back to our sailor shack for review. Having seen EC with Cream &amp; Blind Faith the previous 2 years, we were mad for this new release. Nothing kicked in until Keep On Growing, unleashing the hounds at 2:20. Yeah, it wasnt Crossroads or even Presence of the Lord, but it had the sound of a thousand guitars. We grabbed tickets for their upcoming concert at the HIC, but sadly, Erics Mom passed away and they cancelled, never to return. When Live at the Fillmore came out, I couldnt get enough of Got to Get Better, followed by the 1st Crossroads LP with Evil &amp; Snake Lake Blues. That band had become a proper mess. But, to this day, LAOALS remains in my rotation  iTunes, car set, etc. Lets see. Desert island LP  LAOALS, Electric Ladyland,.. Hard to chose just one.
             </p>
            </section>

              <time datetime="2015-03-06T18:59:33+00:00">
               March 6, 2015 at 6:59 pm
              </time>
             </a>
             <a class="comment-reply-link" href="/real-history-layla?replytocom=9094#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm("comment-9094", "9094", "respond", "8437")'>
              Reply
             </a>
             
            </header>
            <!-- .comment-meta -->
            <section class="comment-content comment">
             <p>
              Have had the master for this work for over 10 years now. Its amazing how much of Duanne Allmans work, was cut out of the original cutting ,,along with the cuttings afterwards. I can only think the reason was, that management , didnt want Clapton over shadowed. Allmans work was incredible in the original cutting,,but more so , if you can get a chance to hear from the master cut.
              <br/>
              Picked this work out in the spring of 72,,along with Eat A Peach and Live at the Filmore,,was the greatest fan of Clapton,,until I got to see Allman live  on 2 occasions in 71RIP Duane Allman..best blues and jazz player there ever was in my book
              <br/>
              YEP !
             </p>
            </section>
            <!-- .comment-content -->
           </article>

          <li class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1" id="li-comment-21837">
           <article class="comment clearfix" id="comment-21837">
            <header class="comment-meta comment-author vcard">
             <div class="comment-avatar">
              <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-75 photo" height="75" src="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/f6eafda9b64c5eeb81d4c125fa2f68ee?s=75&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F1.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D75&amp;r=G" width="75"/>
             </div>
             <strong>
              Robert
             </strong>
             <br/>
             <a class="comment-meta-time muted-small" href="http://www.udiscovermusic.com/real-history-layla#comment-21837">
              <time datetime="2015-05-26T05:16:26+00:00">
               May 26, 2015 at 5:16 am
              </time>
             </a>
             <a class="comment-reply-link" href="/real-history-layla?replytocom=21837#respond" onclick='return addComment.moveForm("comment-21837", "21837", "respond", "8437")'>
              Reply
             </a>
             
            </header>
            <!-- .comment-meta -->
            <section class="comment-content comment">
             <p>
              In the early 70s I was living in Oak Park, Sacramento in a crash pad with by brother and some other dudes.  I drove everybody crazy with the Allman Bros., Cream, Clapton, then Layla over and over.  But I owned the record player, so I was in control until someone took the stylus.  Saw Clapton with Delaney and Bonnie. Dallas Pop Festival 1969 and Derek and the Dominoes in Sacramento (Memorial Auditorium)  late 70 early 71 with a long, beautiful Layla. those were the days, yes they were.
             </p>
            </section>

       <div class="footer-widget clearfix" id="text-84">
        <h3 class="widget-title">
         What is uDiscover?
        </h3>
        <div class="textwidget">
         uDiscover is a brand new way to find out more about music from Universal; with the latest news, full length features written by our crack team of writers, curated playlists, hard-to-find videos, in depth artist pages and regular big prize competitions, it'll enable you to explore albums and songs from our roster of legendary artists.
         <br/>
         Sign up for regular updates spanning a variety of genres to help you dig a little deeper into the music you love.
        </div>
       </div>

  <script type="text/javascript">
   /* &lt;![CDATA[ */
				jQuery.noConflict()(function($){
					$(document).ready(function(){
						var sticky_navigation_offset_top = $('#mainmenu-block-bg').offset().top+0;
						var sticky_navigation = function(){
							var scroll_top = $(window).scrollTop(); // our current vertical position from the top

							if (scroll_top &gt; sticky_navigation_offset_top) {
																	$('#mainmenu-block-bg').css({ 'position': 'fixed', 'top':0, 'left':0, 'z-index':11 });
															} else {
								$('#mainmenu-block-bg').css({ 'top':0, 'position': 'relative','padding-top':0 });
							}
						};

						// run our function on load
						sticky_navigation();

						// and run it again every time you scroll
						$(window).scroll(function() {
							sticky_navigation();
						});
					});
				});
			/* ]]&gt; */
  </script>
  <script type="text/javascript">
   /* &lt;![CDATA[ */

	// Masonry
	jQuery.noConflict()(function($){
		$(document).ready(function() {

			$.cookieBar();

			var $container = $('#blog-entry');

			$container.imagesLoaded(function(){
				$container.masonry({
					itemSelector: '.masonry-box',
					isAnimated: true,
									});
			});

	// Infinite Scroll

	$container.infinitescroll({
		navSelector  : '.pagination',    // selector for the paged navigation
		nextSelector : '.pagination a',  // selector for the NEXT link (to page 2)
		itemSelector : '.masonry-box',     // selector for all items you'll retrieve
		loading: {
			finishedMsg: ( typeof ct_localization != 'undefined' || ct_localization != null ) ? ct_localization.no_posts : "No more posts to load.",
			img: 'http://www.udiscovermusic.com/wp-content/themes/wp-rule/img/ajax-loader.gif'
		}
	},

	// trigger Masonry as a callback
	function( newElements ) {
		var $newElems = $( newElements ).css({ opacity: 0 });

		$newElems.imagesLoaded(function()   {
			$newElems.animate({ opacity: 1 });
			$container.masonry( 'appended', $newElems, true );

			// post like system
			$(".post-like a").click(function() {

				heart = $(this);
				post_id = heart.data("post_id");

				$.ajax({
						type: "post",
						url: ajax_var.url,
						data: "action=post-like&amp;nonce="+ajax_var.nonce+"&amp;post_like=&amp;post_id="+post_id,
						success: function(count){
							if(count != "already") {
								heart.addClass("voted");
								heart.siblings(".count").text(count);
							}
						}
				});
				return false;
			}) // end post like system

		});
	});

 

	});
});
/* ]]&gt; */
  </script>
